THE COMMISSION 
Purpose and Powers 
The Commission is a public instrumentality, a body po litic and corporate and a political subdivision of the 
Commonwealth, separate and apart from the City.  It was cr eated by the passage by the Massachusetts Legislature of 
the Act in July 1977 in response to a “home rule” petition of the City.  In August 1977, the ownership, operation and 
control of the City’s Systems were transferred to the Co mmission.  The Act authorizes  the Commission to operate, 
maintain and construct all necessary improvements to the Systems, establish and collect rates and charges for its services 
(which rates and charges are not subject to regulation by any other governmental body), and finance its operations and 
improvements through revenue collections and the sale of  bonds and notes payable solely from the Commission’s 
revenues.  The Act further provides that any revenue surpluses earned by the Commission in any Fiscal Year shall be 
credited to the next year’s rates or returned to the City.  Since its inception, the Commission has generated a surplus in 
each year of its operations and has credited the surplus to the reduction of the next year’s rates. 
The Act may be amended only by means of further “home rule” petitions of the City enacted by the 
Massachusetts Legislature, or by means of state legislation affecting generally all water and sewer districts within the 
Commonwealth. 
In 1993, the Commonwealth enacted Chapter 40N of the Massachusetts General Laws, the Model Water and 
Sewer Reorganization Act (as amended, the “Model Act”), which permits municipalities to establish independent water 
and sewer commissions.  In addition, it permits existing commissions, such as the Commission, to accept its provisions 
in whole or in part.  The Commission has accepted certain provisions of the Model Act including provisions permitting 
the Commission to self-insure for workers’ compensation and certain provisions which have the effect of now limiting 
any tort liability of the Commission to $100,000 and its liability for defects in a public way to $5,000.  The Commission 
may adopt other provisions of the Model Act in the future.   
Discussion of Management Objectives and Accomplishments 
The Commission was created to maintain and improve the long-term quality and reliability of water and sewer 
services for all users in the City and to assure adequate funding for the operation and maintenance of the Systems.  (As 
noted earlier in this Official Statement, the Systems incl udes the Water Distribution System (including potable water 
and fire suppression facilities), the Sewer System (including separate sanitary sewer, sepa rate storm water drains and 
combined sewers), and the related appurtenances and fixtures.)  The Commission is committed to four primary goals:  
 To maintain and improve the Water Distribution and Sewer Systems.  The Commission is committed 
to a number of improvements to the Water Dist ribution System and the Sewer System, including 
following an aggressive renewal and replacemen t program, reducing unacc ounted-for water, 
encouraging conservation and improving the environment. The Commission is also committed to 
meeting or exceeding the requirements of all federal and state water and wastewater laws, regulations 
and technical standards. 
 To establish and administer a billing and collections system that is fair and efficient.  The Commission 
has worked to establish a rate structure that fully reflects its costs, fairly distributes the financial 
obligation concerning its customer base, and enco urages water conservation. The metering, billing 
and collection process is a central focus of th e Commission’s full management team and the 
Commission is committed to maintaining its strong record in that area. 
 To maintain a strong financial structure.  The Commission has consistently employed conservative 
financial projections and budgeting assumptions, maintained adequate reserves and struck a 
reasonable balance between debt funding and rate funding of capital expenses. 
 To sustain the effectiveness of investments and comply with regulations.  The Commission is 
committed to complying with all its regulatory obligations under federal and state laws, including the 
Safe Drinking Water Act (“SDWA ”) and the Clean Water Act (“CWA”) with its National Pollutant 
Discharge Elimination System (“NPDES”) permitti ng obligations for both stormwater systems and 
combined sewer systems.  Compliance obligations also extend to meeting and exceeding the goals 
and requirements of the Boston Harbor Decree and the Consent Decree executed in 2012 with the 
Environmental Protection Agency (“EPA”) related to stormwater discharges.  See LITIGATION.   
20 
The Commission has made significant progress toward the achievement of these objectives.  Some of the 
Commission’s accomplishments are outlined below: 
 The Commission has realized an op erating surplus in every year since its inception as a result of its 
strong fiscal controls and conservative budgeting practices.  These surpluses are used to stabilize rates 
in each subsequent year.  In Fiscal Year 2020, the Commission is projecting an unaudited surplus of 
approximately $430,000. This surplus reflects the use of $19.0 million in withdrawals from the Rate 
Stabilization Fund during the period of the COVID-19 pandemic.  See HISTORICAL REVENUES 
AND EXPENSES. 
 The Commission has an effective collection program that utilizes automated dunning letters, telephone 
calls, water service terminations for delinquent acco unts, litigation, tax takings and foreclosures. To 
help customers facing financial challenges cau sed by the pandemic, the Commission temporarily 
suspended water service terminations and delinquency charges. For calendar 2021 the Commission 
reinstated all collection activities other than service terminations. 
 Over a number of years, the Commission has been able to contribute to the Stabilization Fund to 
mitigate future rate increases.  As of December 31, 2020, the unaudited balance in the Stabilization 
Fund was approximately $42.6 million. 
 In 2018, the Commission received the Operations  & Environmental Perfor mance Award from the 
National Association of Clean Water Agencies (“ NACWA”) This award is presented to member 
agencies for outstanding initiatives in wastewater  treatment, wastewater conveyance, stormwater 
management, and/or environmental enhancement. The award recognizes an innovative and effective 
project, system or method relating to wastewater  treatment plant or collection system operations 
developed and successfully implemented by a NACWA member in a cost-effective manner while 
achieving environmental compliance objectives. 
 In 2018, the Commission received the Association of Metropolitan Water Agencies Sustainable Water 
Utility Management Platinum Award. The Sustainable Water Utility Management Award recognizes 
water utilities that have made a commitment to management that achieves a balance of innovative and 
successful efforts in areas of economic, social and environmental endeavors. The Platinum and Gold 
Awards recognize outstanding achievement in implementing the nationally recognized Attributes of 
Effective Utility Management. 
 In 2018, the Commission was ranked highest in customer satisfaction in the Northeast by J.D. Power 
in its 2018 Water Utility Residential Customer Sa tisfaction Study. The stud y measures satisfaction 
among residential customers of 88 water utilities delivering water to a population of at least 400,000 
people and is reported in four geographic regions: Midwest, Northeast, South and West. Overall 
satisfaction is measured by examin ing attributes within six factors (listed in order of importance): 
delivery; price; conservation; billing and payment; communications; and customer service. 
 In 2019, the Commission received The Regional Recognition Award from MassDEP for outstanding 
performance and achievements. The Commission was recognized as a model public water agency that 
puts forth exemplary efforts in every area of water treatment and distribution including the Lead 
Service Line Replacement Program. 
 In 2019, the Commission was presented with th e Commonwealth of Massachusetts Governor’s 
Citation in recognition of dedicated service to maintaining a safe and abundant supply of clean water 
and commitment to protecting the environment. This award recognizes organizations which provide 
meaningful contributions which distinguish them fro m their peers. These awards focus attention on 
consistent, positive achievements by both individuals and teams of employees, and recognize those 
who demonstrate: innovation and dedication to their work, concern for the public trust and a 
commitment to excellence. 
 In 2020, the Commission received The Massachuse tts Department of Environmental Protection’s 
(MassDEP) Drinking Water Program for outstanding performance in 2019. The Commission achieved 
one of the top compliance scores in the Consecutive System category of the 2020 Public Water System 
Awards Program. 
 In 2020, the Commission’s Current Expense Budg et document received th e Government Finance 
Officers Association’s (“GFOA”) Distinguished Budget Award, the highest form of recognition in  
21 
governmental budgeting. This award recognized BWSC’s Current Expense Budget met the GFOA 
program criteria as a policy document, an operation guide, a financial plan and a communication 
device. 
 In 2020, the Commission received the GFOA’s Certificate of Achievement for Excellence in Financial 
Reporting for its Comprehensive Annual Financial Report (“CAFR”). The CAFR was judged based 
on its conformity to Generally Accepted Accounting Principles (“ GAAP”) and its compliance with 
other finance related legal and contractual provisions. The Certificate of Achievement is the highest 
form of recognition in governmental accounting and financial reporting and its attainment represents 
a significant accomplishment for any municipality or governmental agency. 
Membership of the Commission 
A three-member Board of Commissioner s (the “Board”), appointed by the Mayor of the City (the “Mayor”), 
subject to confirmation by the City Council, oversees the Commission’s activities.  The Board’s primary responsibility 
is to ensure the sound, economical an d efficient maintenance of the Systems and the provision of water and sewer 
services to City residents.  The Board currently meets on ce a month.  The Act provides that Board members shall be 
appointed to serve four-year staggered terms and must maintain residency within the City.  Any Board member may be 
reappointed; any member may be removed by the Mayor for cause at any time subject to a public hearing procedure.  
At least one Board member must have extensive experience in finance and accounting and one member must have 
extensive experience in the field of administration or busi ness.  Members of the Board are not compensated for their 
services, but are reimbursed for expenses necessarily incu rred in the performance of th eir Commission duties. If the 
Mayor does not fill a vacancy on the Board within 90 days (or in the case of an unexpired term, within 60 days), the Act 
provides that such vacancy may be filled by majority vote of the City Council.

Organization and Staff 
The Commission’s organizational structure consists of five distinct operating divisions.  The following 
describes the major functions of each of the Commission’s major organizational divisions. 
The Executive Division provides executive management, including policy formation and strategic planning, to the entire 
Commission.  The Executive Division also represents the Commission in all legislative-lobbying efforts pertaining to 
securing continued federal and state funded rate relief.  This Division is also responsible for implementing and 
monitoring the Commission’s affirmative action plans and ensuring the participation of women and minority owned 
businesses in obtaining goods and services contracts and safety management to the entire Commission. Additional 
responsibilities include representing the Commission in all litigation and overseeing the Commission.  The Executive 
Division also provides its employees with proactive personnel services and is responsible for the safety and training of 
employees. 
 
The Operations Division ensures the ongoing maintenance and emergency repairs to the Commission’s water and sewer 
mains, service connections, hydrants and oversees pump stations for water, sewers and drainage systems.  The Division 
is also responsible for inventory control, management , and maintenance of the au tomotive fleet for the entire 
Commission. 
 
The Engineering Division is responsible for high quality, reliable water and sewer and drainage services.  This is 
achieved by effectively planning, designing, managing and providing contract compliance for the construction of the 
Commission’s Capital Improvement Programs. 
 
The Administration Division provides the Commission and the Commission’s customers with administrative support 
services.  This Division is responsible for representin g the Commission at various public and community meetings, 
maintaining collections, customer services and the installatio n and repair of meters, and the provision of facilities and 
support services.  The Administration Di vision is also responsible for the preservation of the Commission’s technical 
infrastructure. 
 
The Finance Division provides effective management of the Commission’s revenues and all its resources.  The Finance 
Division accomplishes this through its financial budgetin g, rate setting, accounting and cash management, billing and 
adjustments.  This Division is also responsible for debt financing, investment management, providing procurement, 
investigation, documentation and resolution of meter problems and erratic consumption.  
23

WATER AND SEWER SYSTEMS 
The Commission’s Water Distribution System and Se wer System service approx imately 90,000 accounts in 
the City.  The Commission’s customer base includes the City’s residential population, its businesses and industries, and 
its public, private and non-profit institutions.  See FINANCIAL OPERATIONS - “Customer Base.”   
Water Distribution System 
The Water Distribution System supplies water to the City through five independent networks based on pressure 
needs and geographical location.  Approximately 90% of the water consumed in the City is distributed through two of 
these networks, the Southern High and the Southern Low.  The Water Distribution System consists of the following: 
Water Main City-Wide 
Appurtenances Pressure Zone (Miles) 
Total Linear Feet 5,323,872 Hydrants 12,724 High Pressure Fire System 15 
Total Linear Miles 1,007 MWRA Meters 29 Northern High 4 
  Valves 17,635 Northern Low 89 
    Southern Extra High 80 
    Southern High 560 
    Southern Low 259 
The Water Distribution System has been designed with redundant capacity to support most of the major 
distribution lines. This redundancy permits required mainte nance work on discrete parts of the Water Distribution 
System without impairing the Commission’s ability to provide continuous service throughout the City. 
The Commission purchases its water supply from th e MWRA through 29 active metered connections located 
at various delivery points throughout the Water Distribution System.  The MWRA obtains its water supply from the 
Quabbin Reservoir, the Wachusett Reservoir and the Ware River which have a combined capacity of approximately 490 
billion gallons.  See  MASSACHUSETTS WATER RESOURCES AUTHORITY.  All major watershed areas are 
located in the central and western portions of Massachusetts .  Water is delivered to the metropolitan Boston area from 
the Quabbin and Wachusett Reservoir systems, located approximately 65 and 35 miles west of Boston, respectively, 
through aqueducts to the Weston and Norumbega Reservoirs and then through MWRA transmission tunnels and pipes 
into the Commission’s five service networks.  The Quabbin Reservoir is located at an elevation approximately 530 feet 
above the mean elevation of the City.  This elevation differential creates a natural gravitational flow through most of the 
MWRA’s waterworks system and thereb y insulates the MWRA, and consequently the Commission, from the cost of 
energy which would otherwise be needed to pump the water to the Water Distribution System. 
In 2011, the Commission completed a Water Distribution Study, which provided a thorough understanding of 
the water system and how to best manage it.  The study gave all stakeholders a better sense of the history of Boston’s 
water infrastructure and provided key insights into current conditions.  The study has been responsible for the 
development of best management practices in value mainte nance, main flushing and more effective methodology of 
selecting pipe to be replaced  under the Capital improvement Plan. Not only do these tools assist current Commission 
employees with an understanding of the system, they prov ide future employees with a roadmap for optimal system 
maintenance.  
The three-year study was divided into two phases.  The first phase was to have both Commission staff and a 
hired contractor collect 93 pipe samples (coupons).  The samples were taken from every neighborhood and varied in 
size and pipe material.  The age of the water pipes ranged from 18 years old to 147 years old and included cast iron, 
ductile iron and a mix of cement lined and unlined.  The pipes and soil sample which were also taken from the location 
were analyzed.  The analysis consisted of physical ob servations and measurements, microscopic examination, 
mechanical properties test, and chemical composition analys is.  The soil sample obtained at each location was also 
analyzed to determine its corrosiveness.  The results of the tests performed were matched with locations on Commission 
maps, and corrosion rates were correlated with fill areas, electrified rail locations and groundwater levels. 
The second phase was to create a sustainable distribution system by developing a risk-based measure for 
selecting pipe to be included in the annual CIP budget.  Th is phase employed sophisticated models that levered all the 
data collected in phase one, data the Commission collects in  its GIS, Citiworks, and hydra ulic model to determine the  
26 
appropriate level of pipe rehabilitation and replacement needed to achieve the Commission’s goals.  The pipe selected 
is based on a risk-based assessment.  The framework is derived from asset management principles, which consider the 
highest risk of pipe to be the probability of a failure, and what the consequences of an event occurring could be (hospital 
flooding or critical customers without water). 
This system is used in the selection of pipes for re placement under the Capital Improvement Program.  Pipes 
with the highest ranking are considered fi rst.  Pipes are also selected within pr oximity to other selected pipe to create 
geographic based contracts.  All street excavations are coordinated with City and State Paving Programs. 
This study recommended that the Co mmission undertake an average 11 miles per year of replacement and 
relining projects through the year 2030.  This ranking sy stem was updated in 2016 resu lting in a recommendation to 
replace and rehabilitate eight miles of pi pe per year. Using the latest technol ogy solutions, BWSC is identifying new 
areas where sustainable improv ements can be made to improve the quality  of our environment and services. With 
aggressive leak detection and repair combined with progressive metering programs, the Commission continues to reduce 
its unbilled and unaccounted-for-water.  These programs have resulted in a reduction in unbilled water by approximately 
87% from 70 mgd in Fiscal Year 1977 to 9.1 mgd in Fiscal Year 2020.  Unbilled water is the difference between water 
purchased from MWRA and water sold to customers.  Of the 9.1 mgd of unbilled water in Fiscal Year 2020, 
approximately 4.3 mgd was identified as water used for unbilled public purposes such as firefighting and street cleaning.  
Thus, unaccounted-for-water was approximately 4.8 mgd, or about 8.2% of the 58.4 mgd which the Commission 
purchased from the MWRA.  The Commission continues to perform a leakage survey of the entire system each year. 
The Commission’s metering programs consist of activities generated through the maintenance and repair of its 
90,000 water meters and its meter reading system.  The Meter Services Department, repairs, installs/replaces meters and 
meter reading devices.  The department’s large meter te sting program follows American Water Work Association 
standards.  Utilizing the data from its meter reading syst em, recommendations are also made to down-size meters to 
more accurately account for low flows.  See CAPITAL IMPROVEMENT PROGRAMS OF THE COMMISSION 
– “Support Projects” – “Metering.” 
Sewer System 
The Commission’s Sewer System collects wastewater and stormwater in the City.  The Sewer System consists 
of the following: 
Sewer Pipes City-Wide 
Appurtenances Sewer System (Miles) 
Total Linear Feet 8,112,171 Catch Basins 30,321 Combined Sewer 144 
Total Linear Miles 1,536 Manholes 50,605 Combined Sewer Overflow 12 
Pumping Stations 18 Outfalls 273 Sanitary Sewer 710 
  Regulators 148 Storm Drain 670 
  Tide Gates 201   
 
 
[Remainder of page intentionally left blank.] 
 
  
27 
The Commission’s sanitary and combined flows are transported to the MWRA’s secondary treatment plant on 
Deer Island.  The backbone of the Commission’s Sewer System is a number of major interceptors which convey flows 
from the Commission’s system to the MWRA’s interceptors. The interceptors and the neighborhoods they serve are as 
follows: 
Interceptor Neighborhood Served 
Boston Main Interceptor South End, Downtown, Roxbury and North Dorchester 
Dorchester Interceptor Dorchester and Neponset 
East Side Interceptor Downtown and North End 
Faneuil Street Trunk Sewer Allston/Brighton 
Roslindale Interceptor Roslindale and West Roxbury 
South Boston Interceptors South Boston 
Southwest Corridor Interceptor Roxbury and Jamaica Plain 
Stony Brook Interceptor Roxbury 
West Side Interceptor Back Bay, Beacon Hill and West End 
Talbot Avenue High Level Sewer Dorchester, Mattapan and Roslindale 
Hyde Park Trunk Sewer Hyde Park 
East Boston Lower Level Sewer East Boston 
Dorchester High Level Sewer Mattapan and Hyde Park 
In early 2009, the Commission completed an internal inspection of its large interceptors.  No major issues with 
the condition of the interceptors were found;  however, some of the interceptors were found to be in need of cleaning.  
Two multi-year contracts were developed to remove all sedime nts from these interceptors.  Both contracts have been 
completed. 
Combined flows that exceed the capacity of the interceptors during storm events discharge into Boston Harbor 
and the Charles River.  In the past several years, the Commission has undertaken a number of studies of its combined 
sewer system and has developed flows to significantly reduce CSO.  The issue of infiltration and inflow (“I/I”) into the 
sanitary system in separated areas of the system is also being addressed.  Surveys have been performed to identify I/I 
sources, stormwater into the sanitary sewer system and programs have been developed and implemented to remove the 
I/I from the sanitary sewers.  Since 1988 to present, there has been an estimated peak I/I reduction of 995.2 mgd in the 
Sewer System.   
In cooperation with the MWRA, the Commission has completed six sewer separation projects in the City that 
were included in the MWRA’s CSO Facilities Plan (the  “MWRA/BWSC CSO Plan”).  Combined, these projects 
involved the installation of approximately 43 miles of new storm drains to provide sewer separation.  These projects 
were funded by the MWRA under a United States Environmental Protection Agency (the “EPA”) court ordered mandate 
to reduce or eliminate CSOs from the Charles River and area beaches.  This work had the dual positive effect of reducing 
CSOs and inflow into the Sewer System, resulting in subs tantial cost savings to the Commission.  The Commission 
continues to separate additional combined sewers using its own funding and has completed all MWRA CSO projects, 
but continues to identify, plan, and execute sewer separation projects in order to reduce CSO’s and I/I into the Sewer 
System throughout the City. See CAPITAL IMPROVEMENT PROGRAMS OF THE COMMISSION – “The 
Sewer System – Sewer Separation and Storm Drainage Program.”  
MassDEP, in conjunction with the MWRA and its Local Bodies, has implemented a program to help remove 
stormwater I/I from the sewer system.  In addition, because private developments may add substantial flows to the sewer 
collection, requiring additional MWRA treatment, MassDEP recommends to the Massachusetts Environmental Policy 
Act Office and the Executive Office of En vironmental Affairs that new developmen ts be required to remove I/I from 
the sanitary sewer system, as part of the requirements by the Secretary of Environmental Affairs.  
28 
Currently MassDEP is using a ratio of 4:1 for I/I removal to new wastewater added.  For example, if a proposed 
project’s calculated new daily wastewater flow is 100,000 gallons per day (“gpd”), the developer is responsible for the 
removal of 400,000 gpd of I/I from the sewer system. 
The Engineering Customer Service Department will not ify the Planning Department of the most current 
estimated wastewater flow that has been submitted by a developer.  The developer is required to either remove sources 
of I/I or make a requisite monetary contribution. 
IMPACT OF ENVIRONMENTAL REGULATION 
The water distribution, wastewater collection and stormwater drainage services provided by the Commission 
are impacted, directly or indirectly, by the requirements of federal and state environmental laws and regulations.  The 
MWRA’s assessments to its Local Bodies, including the Co mmission, include the substantial costs of complying with 
the mandates of the CWA under the orders of the United Stat es District Court for the Dist rict of Massachusetts (the 
“District Court”) in a Federal CWA enforcement action brought against the MWRA and others relating to Boston Harbor 
(the “Clean Water Act Case”).  See LITIGATION.  The Commission’s capital improvement programs and financial 
operations also reflect the impact of environmental oversight and regulation of the Commission’s own operations.  
Various environmental issues that have affected and are expected to affect the Commission’s services and operating or 
capital costs in upcoming years are discussed below. 
Water Distribution 
Under the federal SDWA, as amended in 1986 and reauthorized and amended in 1996, the EPA regulates the 
level of contaminants allowed in drinking water of most water supply systems in the United States, including the 
MWRA’s waterworks system and the Commission’s Water Distribution System, by establishing national drinking water 
standards so that drinking water will be protected against microbiological or chemical contaminants. Standards include 
maximum levels for contaminants or treatment techniques and other performance standards. Enforcement of drinking 
water standards in Massachusetts under the SDWA has been delegated to MassDEP which also acts under authority of 
state law and has adopted Massachusetts Drinking Water Standards.  The state and federal rules implementing the 1986 
amendments to the SDWA impose additional treatment re quirements for surface water sources, including criteria, 
procedures and timetables for state determinations of whether filtration measures were required.  The amendments also 
established maximum permissible levels of coliform bacteria occurrence in dist ribution systems, and the testing and 
control of lead and copper in water at the customers’ taps. 
When the SDWA was amended and reauthorized in 1996, the water quality standard setting process was 
revised, a revolving loan fund for drinking water projects  was established, water supp liers were required to issue 
consumer confidence reports, and a timetable was established for further regulation of microbial pathogens in drinking 
water and potentially harmful disinfection by-products.  As part  of such further regulation, the first set of rules, the 
Interim Enhanced Surface Water Treatment Rule and Stage 1 Disinfectants and Disinfec tion By-products Rule were 
issued in December 1998, and became effective in November 2001.  These rules specify further treatment requirements 
for filtered systems to protect against pathogens and revise the maximum contaminant levels for potentially harmful 
disinfection by-products. 
Between June 1993 and October 2005, the MWRA worked under an administrative consent order entered into 
with the Massachusetts Department of Conservation and Recreation (“DCR”), formerly the Metropolitan District 
Commission (“MDC”) and MassDEP, to comply with the SDWA’s 1989 Surface Water Treatment Rule and other rules 
under the 1986 amendments.  The SDWA rules and the consent order included obligations to construct a new water 
treatment plant and install covered water storage facilities at Norumbega Reservoir, Weston Reservoir, Spot Pond, and 
Fells Reservoir.  Based on an annual review, MassDEP continues to find that the MWRA’s water supply meets federal 
criteria for unfiltered sources. 
With the addition of ultraviolet (UV) disinfection in April 2014, the John Carroll Water Treatment Plant now 
complies with all rules currently in force, including the Long Term 2 Enhanced Surface Water Treatment Rule and the 
Stage 2 Disinfection Byproducts Rule. 
In 1991, the EPA issued the Lead and Copper Rule (the “Lead Rule”) regulating the concentration of lead and 
copper in drinking water. Lead enters tap water through the corrosion, or wearing away of lead contained in service 
piping, solder used in plumbing, and some brass fixtures.  The Commission’s drinking water is treated at the MWRA’s 
John Carroll facility to make it less corrosive.  Under the Lead Rule, water suppliers must conduct lead and copper 
sampling programs, identify and implement optimal corrosion control treatment, and provide information to the public  
29 
on ways to further reduce their exposure to lead in drinking water.  At the time the Lead Rule was passed, the MWRA 
and MassDEP agreed that, since the MWRA  was a consecutive water system and provides the same treated drinking 
water to all the communities it serves, the number of l ead and copper samples the MWRA communities were required 
to collect could be reduced.  The Commission collects lead and copper samples from the required 25 customer locations 
and submits them to the MWRA.  The MWRA analyzes the samples and provides the results from all the MWRA Local 
Bodies to MassDEP. 
In March 2004, the Commission’s sample results exceede d the 90th percentile lead action level, triggering a 
series of required actions under the Lead Rule, including the conducting of a public education program and the 
implementation of an approved Lead Service Line Re placement Program. The Commissi on’s Lead Service Line 
Replacement Program was approved by MassDEP in November  2004. Over a three-year period from 2004-2007, the 
Commission removed 1,074 public lead service lines identi fied in the system, far exceeding MassDEP requirement to 
remove 107 service lines annually.  In February 2008, as a result of consecutive, favorable water sampling results below 
the 90th percentile lead action level, both MassDEP and EPA allowed the Commission to suspend its Lead Service Line 
Replacement Program.  The Commission, however, continued to  replace lead lines in the public way as they are 
encountered through routine maintenance. 
In accordance with state and federal regulations, the Commission continues its sampling program at 25 private 
sites that are known to have lead services.  Importantly, Boston’s drinking water is lead free when it leaves the reservoirs, 
and the MWRA and Boston’s water mains do not contain lead.  In October 2020, the Commission’s sample results from 
the identified private homes exceeded the 90th percentile lead action level for the first time in over a decade, triggering 
a series of required actions under the Lead Rule. In compliance with regulatory requirements, the Commission has 
implemented a robust public education campaign to inform particularly vulnerable members of the public about the 
dangers of lead in water. The Commission has updated its Lead Service Line Replacement Program, which was approved 
by MassDEP in March 2021. The Commission is actively re moving known lead service lines in the system and 
investigating service lines of an unknown composition. The EPA has published changes to the Lead Rule, which upon 
implementation at a future date uncertain, will require systems to remove all lead lines in the public way and also those 
located on private property. Consistent with this pending EPA rule change and MassDEP requirements, the Commission 
is actively pursuing removal of all lead service lines in the system, regardless of  their status as public or privately-
owned. 
Under the Commission’s water use regulations, the home or property owner is responsible for the private 
portion of the water service line, which is the portion running from the property line into the basement or to the meter.  
The cost of private service line replacement is therefore the responsibility of the individual home or property owner. In 
2004, the Commission created the Lead Replacement Incentive Program.  The program was designed to encourage the 
replacement of private lead water service lines in the City by  providing financial assistance to eligible homeowners to 
help defray and finance the cost of lead service line replacements.  In February 2016, the Board of Commissioners voted 
to upgrade the Commission’s existing Lead Replacement Incentive Program increasing the credit from $1,000 to $2,000, 
while expanding eligibility to all properties with services 2” and under. In March 2021, the Board of Commissioners 
voted to further upgrade this credit from $2,000 to $4,000 to  incentivize property owners to remove their private lead 
service lines and reduce the financial cost on them to do so. The Commission is preparing a mailing to all customers 
with a known private lead service line or a private service line of unknown composition to inform them of the increased 
credit and public health benefits of lead service line re moval.  The Commission does not expect that the SDWA 
requirements will impose any significant additional burden of lead service line replacement in excess of its current 
program.   
The SDWA requires that all community water systems publish an annual drinking water quality report to be 
distributed to all customers of each community system.  The report, called a Consumer Confidence Report, is required 
to contain monitoring results of all detected contaminants that are regulated by the EPA.  The regulations governing this 
provision of the SDWA were promulgated in August 1998.  The report has been published annually since 1998 by the 
MWRA, in cooperation with the communities it serves. 
The Public Health Security and Bioterrorism Response Act, enacted in June 2002, mandated the preparation of 
a Vulnerability Assessment and Emergency Response Plan by each public drinking water supplier.  The Commission’s 
Vulnerability Assessment was submitted and received by th e EPA in March 2003.  The Commission’s Emergency 
Response Plan was completed in September 2003, certification of its completion was submitted to the EPA in September 
2003 and it was updated in July 2004, January 2009, November 2011, and December 2014.  The Emergency Response 
Plan addresses the actions to be taken in response to major or catastrophic events and terrorist attacks on the 
Commission’s Water Distribution System.  Based in part upon the findings of the Vulnerability Assessment and the  
30 
Emergency Response Plan, the Commission continues to design and develop Water Distribution System improvements 
to mitigate, prevent, detect and respond to disruptive acts and terrorist activities. 
Combined Sewer Overflows 
The Federal Clean Water Act authorizes CSO discharges through a National Pollutant Discharge Elimination 
System Permit (“NPDES Permit”), issued jointly in Massachusetts by the EPA and MassDEP. 
The Commission currently has 37 active CSOs listed on its NPDES Permit, significantly less than the number 
of CSOs contained in the Commission’s initial NPDES Perm it.  In March 2003, the EPA and MassDEP issued the 
Commission an NPDES Permit for the 26 CSOs on Schedule A and the 11 CSOs on Schedule B. The Commission’s 
NPDES Permit expired on March 27, 2008.  As required, the Commission submitted an application to renew its NPDES 
Permit on November 26, 2007 and is waiting for a response to the request.  (Although the NPDES Permit “expired” in 
March 2008, the terms of the old NPDES Permit remain in effect until a new NPDES Permit is issued.) 
The NPDES Permit allows the Commission to discharge combined sewage only during wet weather; discharges 
during dry weather are prohibited.  A number of duties are required to be performed such as inspections and maintenance 
of the combined system, monitoring and annual reporting of CSO activity, notifying the public, and participation in the 
MWRA’s long term control plan for CSOs which was approved in 1997. 
The MWRA developed its long-term CSO control plan over the course of several years in the 1990s.  The 
MWRA identified a number of relatively easy to install improvements for the Commission to implement while the long-
term CSO control plan was being developed.  Over 100 of  these improvements, referred to as system optimization 
projects, were implemented by the Commission using its own staff.  Commission staff raised weirs and blocked 
overflows, with the effect of reducing or eliminating discharges at many locations.  This in turn had the effect of reducing 
future CSO controls, making it possible to eliminate more co stly controls such as storage and hydraulic relief for the 
combined areas in downtown Boston and the combined areas along Dorchester Avenue in South Boston. 
In 1997, the MWRA’s long-term CSO control plan reco mmended separating flows with combined sewers in 
certain areas of the City.  The Commission accepted the responsibility for managing both the design and the construction 
to separate sewers in these areas. As of June 30, 20 16, the Commission has completed all MWRA CSO separation 
projects throughout the City. These combined projects, funded by the MWRA under an EPA court ordered mandate to 
reduce or eliminate CSOs from the Charles River and area beaches, included the installation of approximately 43 miles 
of new storm drains to provide sewer separation in these areas, and have also yielded a reduction of CSOs and inflow 
into the sewer system which, consequently, has generated substantial cost savings to the Commission. In conjunction 
with the sewer separation projects, the Commission has also replaced old water mains and services in these areas using 
its own funding. 
The Commission has also implemented a floatable controls program for CSOs, designing and installing 
underflow baffles in 10 regulator structures.  All of the underflow baffles are in place and provide a means of reducing 
the discharge of floatable materials when CSOs occur. 
Stormwater Discharges 
Discharges to the Commission’s separate storm drainage system are regulated under the EPA’s NPDES 
Stormwater Permit Regulations.  The Commission’s NP DES Stormwater Permit MAS010001 (the “Stormwater 
Permit”) became effective on October 29, 1999. 
In accordance with the Stor mwater Permit, the Commission continues to develop and implement stormwater 
pollution prevention and management programs designed to reduce, to the maximum extent practicable, the discharge 
of pollutants to the municipal storm drainage system.  The emphasis of the Commission’s stormwater programs is on 
activities that produce the greatest environmental protection and public health benefits, and which are within the 
Commission’s jurisdiction to control. 
Through a variety of measures and programs the Commission is meeting the requirements of its Stormwater 
Permit.  (Although the Stormwater Permit expired in October 2004, the terms thereof remain in effect until a new one 
is issued.  In March 2004, the Commission submitted an application to the EPA for re-issuance, and discussions between 
the Commission and the EPA regarding the requirements of any re-issued Stormwater Permit are ongoing.)  The 
Commission’s illegal connections remediation program has been in effect since 1986.  Through December 31, 2020, 
1,869 illicit sanitary sewer discharges to storm drains have been eliminated, resulting in the removal of an estimated 
846,000 gallons per day of wastewater from the drainage system and receiving waters.  The Commission regularly  
31 
inspects, cleans and maintains its catch basins citywide.  Cleaning restores the effective capacity of catch basins, thereby 
allowing for greater solids removal from stormwater flows.  Hoods and traps are replaced on catch basins as needed.  
The Commission’s site plan review process has been upgr aded and enhanced in recent years.  The Commission now 
exercises greater control over  non-stormwater discharges, and ensures that construction sites and new development 
projects conform to its requirements, as well as state and federal requirements, for stormwater management. 
FINANCIAL OPERATIONS 
Generally Accepted Accounting Principles 
The Commission is required under the Act to maintain its books and records in accordance with GAAP and to 
have an annual audit performed by an independent certified public accountant or a firm of certified public accountants.  
The Commission has complied with this requirement and ha s received an unmodified au dit opinion each year since 
inception, including the financial statements with audit opinion for the year ended December 31, 2019. See APPENDIX 
C. 
To accommodate the rate making process, the Commi ssion follows the accounting standards set forth in 
Governmental Accounting Standards Board Statement No. 62 (“GASB 62”), “Codification of Accounting and Financial 
Reporting Guidance Contained in Pre-November 30, 1989 FASB and AICPA Pronouncements.”   GASB 62 allows 
certain (a) revenues provided for future allowable costs to  be deferred until the costs ar e actually incurred (deferred 
credits) and (b) costs incurred to be cap italized if recovery is reasonably assu red (deferred charges).  These changes 
allow a more meaningful presentation of  the results of operations in that the financial statements prepared under 
generally accepted accounting principles more closely reflect the Commission’s rate setting policies. 
Rates 
The Commission is required by the Act to set rates and charges to provide revenues at least sufficient to (i) pay 
all current expenses, (ii) pay all debt service, (iii) create and maintain reas onable reserves required by any bond 
resolution, (iv) provide funds for paying the cost of all ne cessary repairs, replacements a nd renewals of the Systems, 
and (v) pay or provide for any and all other amounts which the Commission may be obligated to pay or provide for by 
law or contract, including any contract with, or resolution for the benefit of the holders of its bonds.  Under the MWRA’s 
enabling act, the Commission is authorized to use a rate structure based on a flat rate per volume of water consumed or 
an ascending unit rate based on such volume.  The Commission may not provide any discount for large volume users, 
although certain discounts are required for elderly and disabled customers.   
The Commission reviews the results of its financial operations each year to determine if a surplus or deficit 
was realized.  The surplus or deficit which results from actual operations is reported in the Commission’s audited 
financial statements.  The Act requires the Commission to apply any surplus to offset rates and charges in the succeeding 
year, or to pay the surplus to the City.  The Commission must also recover any deficit by increasing its rates and charges 
in the following year. 
Since the Commission’s inception, th e Commission’s financial operations have generated surpluses in each 
year which have been applied to offset rate increases in each succeeding year.  Results for the past five Fiscal Years for 
which results have been audited are set forth below in the tables entitled “Historical Revenues, Expenses and Fund 
Deposits” and “Historical Net Revenues and Coverage.” 
Currently, the Commission employs an inclining block rate structure, consisting of six steps, designed to 
promote water conservation.  Each step or “block” in the ra te structure is reflective of the average daily use of the 
Commission’s customers as determined by meter size.  The ra te per cubic foot for water and sewer usage increases as 
the amount of water and sewer usage increases.  
Due to an increase in expenditures, the long-term decline in water consumption and the impact on water sales 
and customer payments from the COVID-19 pandemic in Fiscal Year 2020, the Commission increased its water and 
sewer rates by an average of 8.90% on January 1, 2021.  As a result, the average one family customer using 180 gallons 
of water per day in 2021 will be charged approximately $107 per 31-day month, or approximately $1,257 annually.  See 
MASSACHUSETTS WATER RESOURCES AUTHORITY - “MWRA Rates and Charges.”

additional I/I loan in the amount of $7.5 million. 
In 2010, the MWRA Board authorized the LWSAP, a loan program to assist its Local Bodies in the performance 
of water system improvement projects.  The program offers interest-free loans payable over a ten-year period and is 
designed to improve water quality in local distribution systems.  The amount of funds available in the program is $34.3 
million per year with Boston receiving a share of approximately $5.3 million dolla rs.  The loans will be repaid to the 
MWRA over 10-year period.  Loan funds are approved for distribution from MWRA Fiscal Years 2011 through 2023.  33 
As of December 31, 2020, the Commission had received $5 2.6 million under the LWSAP of which $30.9 million is 
outstanding. In May 2021 the Commission closed on an additional LWSAP loan in the amount of $2.3 million.

Projected Revenues and Expenses 
The projections set forth in the tables captioned “Projected Revenues, Expenses and Fund Deposits” and 
“Projected Net Revenues and Coverage” constitute Forward-Looking Statements.  Such projections and statements are 
based on Commission management’s current expectations for Fiscal Years 2020 through 2025, have been reviewed by 
the Commission’s Financial Feasibility Consultant, Amawalk Consulting Group, LLC, and are subject to a number of 
factors and uncertainties, which could cause results to differ materially from those described in such projections and 
statements.  There can be no assurance that actual results or operating conditions will not differ materially from those 
anticipated or suggested in su ch projections and statements, particularly af ter Fiscal Year 2020 as a result of various 
factors, including but not limited to the following:  unantic ipated increases in the Comm ission’s operating expenses; 
unanticipated changes in demand for water; changes in the Commission’s collections rate; regulatory or other changes 
or conditions requiring increased capital expenditures by the MWRA or the Commission; other factors affecting the 
MWRA’s projected assessments to the Commission; changes in  interest rates; economic conditions in the City of 
Boston; and general economic conditions. The effects of the COVID-19 pandemic on each of the above factors in Fiscal 
Years 2021 through 2025 are not known at this time.  See APPENDIX B.

MWRA
 Assessments to the Commission 
Currently, the MWRA supplies wholesale water to local water departments in 54 communities via its 
waterworks system.  The MWRA also pr ovides wastewater treatment to 43 communities which transfer sewage to 
MWRA’s treatment plants on the shor es of Boston Harbor.  The MWRA assesses Local Bodies, including the 
Commission, for its water and sewer services on an annual basis and bills the assessments ten times a year.  Assessments 
for water services are currently computed by the MWRA on the basis of metered water use for the calendar year 
immediately preceding the current MWRA Fiscal Year.  The MWRA sewer rate methodology allocates total operating 
costs based upon the average of the prior two calendar years’ total metered flow with adjustments for strength of flow 
to take into account above-average concentrations of total suspended solids and biochemical oxygen demand.  Capital 
costs, including debt service, are allocated as follows: (i) two-eighths based upon the average of the prior three calendar 
year’s peak month flow (with adjustments for strength of flow), (ii) three-eighths based upon contributing population, 
and (iii) three-eighths based upon census population. See MASSACHUSETTS WATER RESOURCES 
AUTHORITY - “MWRA Rates and Charges.”  
MWRA assessments are budgeted by the Commission as operating expenses, although under the flow of funds 
established by the Resolution payment of such MWRA assessm ents are subordinate to the payment of debt service on 
Bonds, to the payment of other operating expenses and to the maintenance at required levels of reserve funds for debt 
service and other purposes.  See SECURITY FOR THE BONDS - “Flow of Funds.”  MWRA assessments to the 
Commission increased substantially since the inception of th e MWRA, but have moderated to single digit increases 
since 1994 from double digit increases in the years prior to 1994.  MWRA assessments to the Commission represented, 
on a rate basis, approximately 62.9% of the Commission’s total operating expenses in Fiscal Year 2020 and are estimated 
to be approximately 61.6% of the Commission’s expenses in  Fiscal Year 2021.  Accord ing to MWRA projections, its 
charges for MWRA Fiscal Years 2022 to 2025 will increase at a compounded average annual rate of 3.5%. 39 
The MWRA’s actual assessments to the Local Bodies generally have been lower than the MWRA’s original 
projections due to a variety of factors including lower than assumed rates of interest and inflation, changes in the timing 
of its capital program and the receipt of federal and state assistance. See MASSACHUSETTS WATER RESOURCES 
AUTHORITY - “MWRA Capital Improvement Program.”   Due to lowered actual assessments from the MWRA, 
combined with cost co ntrol measures undertaken by the Commission a nd receipt by the Commission of sewer debt 
service assistance from the Commonwealth, the Commission, in turn, has been able to adopt actual rates that are lower 
than earlier projections.  See FINANCIAL OPERATIONS - “Rates”  above.  There can be no assurance that this 
historic trend of the MWRA’s and Commission’s actual assessments and rates being lower than original projections will 
continue.

As illustrated in the above table, water purchases by the Commission have declined generally in the past decade 
and decreased more substantially in Fis cal Year 2020. The Commission attributes  the declines in water purchases to 
overall weather conditions, to the reduction of unaccounted-for-water and to a decline in water sales. Water sales by the 
Commission have also declines over time, from 57.1 mgd in Fi scal Year 2008 to 52.8 mgd in Fiscal Year 2019 and to 
49.4 mgd in Fiscal Year 2020. The Commission attributes th e long-term declines to increased conservation efforts by 
users, (such as low-flow faucets, showerheads, lower capacity toilet tank assemblies and other consumption reducing 
practices). The water sales decline in Fiscal Year 2020 also reflects the effects of the COVID-19 pandemic and resulted 
in a 6.0% decline in water purchases. 
Customer Base 
The Commission provides water distribution, wastewater collection and stormwater drainage services, on a 
retail basis, to residential, commercial, industrial, public, private, and non-profit institutional properties throughout the 
City.  The diversity and stability of the Commission’s customer base represents one of its long-term strengths. 
The Commission’s customer base includes numerous large tax-exempt hospitals, universities, religious 
institutions, governmental agencies, and other institutions that are not included in the City’s property tax base.  As of 
December 31, 2020, such tax-exempt entities accounted for approximately 23.9 % of the Commission’s sales on a 
consumption basis.  Commercial facilities, such as office buildings and hotels, accounted for approximately 21.4% of 
sales, while approximately 2.7% of sales involved industrial users.  Approximately 29.4% of the Commission’s sales 
were generated from single, two, and three family residential properties. Larger, multi-family rental, and condominium 
properties accounted for approximately 22.6% of sales.
