Collecti
ons 
In recent years, the Commission has expanded and strengthened its fully-integrated collections program, which 
includes interest charges on overdue accounts, dunning letters , telephone calls, termination of service, tax takings and 
foreclosures. 
The Commission successfully completed the implementation of a new billing system in September 2019. There 
were no billing interruptions and no significant increase in  customer complaints. With implementation of the UMAX 
billing system, the Commission changed the period an account will become delinquent, from forty-six days to thirty-
one days from the date a bill is posted/printed by th e Commission, at which point delinquency charges of 14% per 
annum are assessed.  The Commission also sends a series of three dunning letters to the owner of the property. Direct 
telephone calls are made via an automated, intelligent call dialing system, in order to obtain payment or establish a 
collection agreement.  This automated dialing system has been integrated with the Commission’s billing and collection 
information systems to provide online account information to telephone coll ectors and a wide variety of management 
reports.  The Commission also has the ability to send SMS texts and email correspondences to its customers. 
Any account with an outstanding balance of more than $250 which is at least sixty (60) days past due can be 
scheduled for termination of service and any account at least six months past due can be scheduled for tax taking.  As 42 
of December 31, 2020, the Commission’s unaudited net receivables was $37,015,075 . In 2020, the Commission 
collected more than $1.2 million through the termination program and the tax taking program. Delinquency and 
termination efforts were suspended on March 10, 2020 due to the COVID-19 pandemic. On January 1, 2021 the 
Commission resumed charging delinquency charges.  Since April 1997, the Commission has contracted with outside 
counsel to take necessary actions to collect against properties with a tax taking status.  In 2020, the Commission collected 
$436,938 in accounts in the Small Claims processing using a variety of methods to collect including letters and notices 
to lenders.   In 2020, the Commission sent Small Claims letters to 273 accounts. 
Information Technology 
Effective use of the right technologies enables BWSC to provide more efficient and high-quality water and 
sewer services to the City.  Strategic planning, careful selec tion of technological tools, and effective use of such tools 
has enabled BWSC to continue to improve the level of service associated with emergency responses, preventative 
maintenance, infrastructure improvements, and most importantly, quality customer service.  Providing staff with 
appropriate training and utilizing the right mix of hardware and software is something BWSC continues to assess and 
act upon as new technologies continue to evolve. 
A number of mission critical software applications are utilized to support BWSC’s daily operations and provide 
for emergency response services 24 hours a day, 7 days a week. BWSC is actively upgrading and replacing various 
systems. The following are projects that have been completed to date and projects projected to be completed during the 
next several years.  
 Replace the Commission’s Building Access Control and CCTV Security systems. This project 
will be
co
mpleted in 2021.
 Upg
rade Engineering Construction Management application. This project will be completed in 2021.
 Upgrade GIS and Asset Management Systems. This project will begin in 2021 and is expected to be
completed in 2022.
Cy
bersecurity: BWSC adheres to the NIST (National Institute of Standards and Technology) Cybersecurity Framework 
(the “NIST Framework”). As the Cybersecurity landscape evolves so does the NIST Framework. The only way to 
accelerate detection and response to sophisticated threats is to understand the behavior of all individual components of 
an attack across your organization. BWSC has selected security products from different vendors which integrate to offer 
an overlapping layered approach to secu rity with multiple levels of protection. In the event of a breach BWSC has 
multiple methods of backups in place to mitigate any loss of data. Most of the deployed security products are now cloud-
based and rely on analysis of large datasets to identify th reats based on patterns. These products also include remote 
monitoring and response. BWSC has deployed security products to cover each functional level of the NIST Framework: 
Identify, Protect, Detect, Respond and Recover. BWSC ha s recently completed a cybersecurity assessment which is 
compliant with the AWIA Cybersecurity and Resilience Assessment requirement.
 
Employee Relations 
The Commission has 502 full-time positions budgeted fo r Fiscal Year 2021.  Positions are organized among 
four bargaining unions and a non-union or executive category.  The number of non-union executive positions is presently 
137. T
he four unions are as follows: the American Federation of State, County and Municipal Employees, Council 93,
Local 804 (“Local 804”) which represents 30 positions; the Service Employees International Union, Local 888 (“Local
88
8”), which represents 43 positions; the Office and Professional Employees International Union, Local 6 (“Local 6”),
which represents 58 positions; and the International Association of Machinists, Local Lodge 100 (“Local Lodge 100”),
which represents 177 positions.
Un
der Chapter 150E of the Massachusetts General Laws, employees of the Commission have certain 
organizational and representational rights which include the right to organize, to bargain collectively by representatives 
of their choice on questions of wages, hours and other terms and conditions of employment, and to engage in lawful 
activities for concerted bargaining or other mutual aid protections.  State law prohibits strikes by Commission 
employees. 
The Commission is party to four collective bargaining agreements (each, an “Agreement”).  The Agreements 
with Local Lodge 100, Local 804 and Local 6 expire on D ecember 31, 2021. Local 888 is engaged in bargaining to 
develop a successor Agreement to the bargaining agreement which expired on June 30, 2020. 43 
Employee Benefits 
Consistent with the terms of the Act, Commission employees who were employed by the City when the 
Commission was established remained members of the State- Boston Retirement System (the retirement system which 
covers most City employees) and all other employees of the Commission became members of such system upon being 
employed by the Commission.  The Commission is required to reimburse the City for its proportionate share of any 
amounts expended by the State-Boston Retirement System for retirement benefits to or on account of Commission 
employees.  The Commission, the City and the system have en tered into certain agreements in connection with such 
reimbursement obligations. 
The Commission uses a retirement trust to fund and manage its liability to reimburse the City.  Actuarial studies 
of the pension liability are performed regularly by Segal Cons ulting, actuarial consultants.  The most recent actuarial 
study was completed in 2021 using information as of December 31, 2020.  As of December 31, 2020, the actuarial 
accrued liability of the Commission for pension payments was $161,527,980 and the actuarial value of the plan assets 
was $132,516,097, resulting in an unfunded actuarial liability as of that date of $29,011,883.  The latter figure reflects 
a $5,796,712 decrease in the unfunded actuarial liability relative to the prior valuation as of December 31, 2019. As of 
December 31, 2020, the market value of the assets in the retirement trust was $132,516,097. 
For 2020, the Commission contributed $2.8 million to the retirement trust. In 2021, the Commission has 
budgeted $3.2 million or approximately 8.0% of payroll fo r pension liabilities and will make additional payments if 
there is an available surplus. The most  recent actuarial study determined that  an annual contribution of approximately 
15% of payroll is needed from 2016 through 2030 to fully fu nd its pension liabilities by the end of 2030.  It should be 
noted that the Commission’s pension fund contributions are in addition to the payroll deductions contributed by 
Commission employees, which range from approximately 5% to 12% per employee, depending upon salary and date of 
hire. 
In addition to pension benefits, the City provides for the retired employees of the Commission certain other 
post-employment benefits (“OPEB”).  The Commission and the City have a cost sharing arrangement under which the 
City will bill the Commission for the premium cost only for retirees of the Commission who are enrolled in the City’s 
group health plan, plus a 2% administrative fee.  Based on the most recent actuarial study completed by Segal Consulting 
as of December 31, 2020, the Commissi on’s unfunded actuarial accrued OPEB liability as of December 31, 2020 was 
$35,868,760, based on an actuarial accrued liability of $66,84 2,443, an actuarial value of assets of $30,973,683 and a 
6.25% per annum discount rate.  The actuarial value of assets represents assets the Commission had designated as OPEB 
assets as of the applicable date.   
The Segal Group actuarial valuation also determined the Annual Required Contribution (“ARC”) to show in 
the Commission’s audited financial statements.  In 2020, the Commission appropriated $3.0 million for OPEB benefits. 
In 2021, consistent with current general policy, it budgeted OPEB Trust contributions at approximately 60% of the ARC 
amount and will make additional payments if there is an ava ilable surplus.  The ARC for 2021 is $4.3 million, and the 
Commission budgeted $3.0 million.  The market value of th e OPEB Trust fund balance as of December 31, 2020 was 
$30,973,683. 
For more information concerning the Commissions post-employment benefits, see the Commission’s 
December 31, 2019 audited financial statements and the notes appended thereto in APPENDIX C. 
CAPITAL IMPROVEMENT PROGRAMS OF THE COMMISSION 
General 
Under the Act and the Resolution, the Commission must annually prepare a three-year Capital Improvement 
Program (“CIP”).  Information generated by the Commission’s ongoing monitoring programs for the Systems and from 
various engineering data files, together  with information concerning the development needs of the City, is used to 
compile a list of pipes, conduits, transmission mains, and other System components to be either renewed, replaced, 
relocated or added to the Systems each year. 
The 2021-2023 CIP was presented by the Commission in November 2020 and was adopted in December 2020. 
The 2021-2023 CIP represents a continued commitment by the Commission to ensure the uninterrupted delivery of  
44 
water and sewer services while working to reduce unnecessary water loss and minimize pollution of Boston Harbor and 
its tributary waters.  A total of approximately $201.1 million in capital expenditures is projected over the three years of 
the CIP, consisting of $87.2 million for water distribution,  $84.3 million for wastewater collection, $9.2 million for 
stormwater, and $20.4 million for support projects. While th ere can be no assurance that projected expenses for the 
projects included in the 2021-2023 CIP will not be exceeded  or that additional projects will not be required, the 
Commission believes the amounts set forth in this section are reasonable for such projects. A copy of the 2021-2023 
CIP is available at the Commission’s website (http://www.bwsc.org). 
In planning its CIPs, the Commission balances the recognized need for ongoing renewal and replacement and 
preservation of its Systems with the desirability of specific improvements to accommodate development or 
redevelopment plans for the City. Wherever feasible, capital improvements are scheduled in cooperation with the street 
rebuilding and reconstruction activities of the City’s Department of Public Works, the Boston Redevelopment Authority, 
and the Massachusetts Highway Department. 
As part of the 20-year Water Distribution Study completed in 2011, all pipes in the BWSC system were placed 
in a ranking system through a computer model utilizing several factors including pipe age, material, soil conditions, 
break history and consequence of failure.  This system is used in the selection of pipes for replacement under the CIP.   
Sewer system evaluation surveys (“SSES”), conducted in several neighborhoods throughout the City, are 
ongoing projects purposed with determining the extent of I/I into the Commission’s sanitary sewer system.  The SSESs 
have resulted in a number of sewer rehabilitation projects, new separated storm drains and downspout disconnection 
contracts. To date, approximately 25,296 downspouts on approximately 10,085 buildings have been disconnected from 
the Commission’s Sewer System. 
In 2014, Massachusetts regulations were revised which required communities to complete an I/I analysis of 
their sewer systems by December 2017. In 2015, the Commi ssion hired a firm to perform a new citywide I/I analysis. 
The analysis was completed in May 2017.  The I/I analysis  report identified areas with excessive I/I as well as 
recommended areas for more detailed analysis in SSESs an d prepared a survey completion schedule that will be 
implemented over 10 years. Further analysis has been completed for Dorchester SSES.  Currently, further analysis is 
under way for Roslindale SSES and Allston Brighton SSES is scheduled next.  
In accordance with the Stormwater Permit, the Commission conducted a program to monitor stormwater quality 
in drainage areas representative of different land uses, including low-density, high-density and commercial areas.  This 
monitoring has been completed. 
In order to meet future user needs and to maintain a high level of service to the Systems’ users, the Commission 
will continue to invest moneys to improve and upgrade exis ting facilities and, in certain cases, develop new facilities.  
Such future capital costs will be financed by the Commission through bond proceeds, federal and state grants or loans, 
operating revenues, or a combination of these or other available sources, including the MWRA.  
The MWRA established programs to assist its 43 sewer service communities with the reduction of I/I.  The I/I 
loan programs provide financial assistance in part with a grant and in part with an interest-free loan payable over a ten-
year period.  The program initially offe red assistance that was 45% grant and 55% loan, and the percentages were 
adjusted in February 2015, to be 75% grant and 25% loan. The Commission will be eligible for $39,494,794 for phases 
nine and ten.  The Commission’s 2021-2023 CIP includes $14.3 million in total financial assistance from the MWRA 
for I/I Grant/Loan Program projects. 
The MWRA’s has also provides support for water systems improvement projects through its LWSAP program.    
The program offers interest-free loans payable over a ten-year period and is designed to improve water quality in local 
distribution systems.  The amount available in the program is $34.3 million per year with Boston receiving a share of 
approximately $5.3 million.  Loans are approved for distri bution from MWRA Fiscal Years 2011 through 2023.  The 
Commission has applied for loan funding for certain water main replacement projects through the remainder of this 
program.  All project costs incurred on or after January 1, 2010 have been considered for eligibility in application under 
the LWSAP.  As of December 31, 2020, the Commission has received $52.6 million in LWSAP funding.  In the 2021-
2023 CIP, $18.1 million will be funded using the LWSAP Program. See WATER AND SEWER SYSTEMS. 45 
The Water Distribution System 
The four primary objectives of the 2021-2023 CIP with respect to the Water Distribution System are to (1) 
ensure a continued adequate supply of high quality, potable water at adequate pressure for consumption by the 
Commission’s customers and for fire protection; (2) reduce the amount of non-revenue producing water and reduce the 
long-term maintenance costs of the system; (3) improve the operability of valves and appurtenances so as to advance 
the efficient operation of the water sy stem; and (4) reduce public inconveniences by coordinating the scheduling of 
system improvements with related projects of other public agencies.  The 2021-2023 CIP has two major programs for 
the Water Distribution System: the Water Main Rehabilitation Program (the “WMR Program”) and the Special Projects 
Program (the “SP Program”).  Each of these programs is summarized below.   
Water Main Rehabilitation ($66.3 million).  The primary purpose of the WMR Program is to ensure the quality 
and quantity of water provided by the Commission to its customers.  Over long periods of time the internal and external 
surfaces of water mains are subject to corrosion and deteriora tion.  Internal corrosion of water mains can affect water 
quality, particularly taste, odor and colo r, as well as reduce the hydraulic capacity of the pipe.  Internal and external 
corrosion can also reduce the structural integrity of pipe, causing potential leakage and main breaks. 
The WMR Program accomplishes the following: 
 Reduces the occurrence of main breaks, public inconvenience, loss of water and associated costs;
 Decreases water leakage;
 Increases the capacity of water mains;
 Reduces discolored water conditions associated with water main tuberculation;
 Reduces long term maintenance costs; and
 Contributes in the control of bio-film in complying with the requirements of the SDWA.
Sp
ecial Projects ($20.9 million). The SP Program includes funding for a variety of system planning and other 
studies, for professional services associated with the rehabilitation and operation of the water system, and for the 
engineering design and construction of the installation or replacement of water mains associated with bridge 
improvement projects undertaken by other agencies.  Also included are the associated design and engineering services 
required for the implementation of capital projects and th e permanent paving fees for ongoing and future capital 
improvements.  Overall, the objectives of the SP Program are to extend the useful life of water mains, reduce long-term 
maintenance and repair costs, reduce the occurrence of main breaks and the resulting impacts, conserve drinking water 
and coordinate improvements with other agencies to minimize disruptions. 
The Sewer System 
The primary objectives of the 2021-2023 CIP with respect to the Sewer System are to provide uninterrupted 
wastewater transport and storm drainage services to the residents, businesses and visitors of Boston and to improve 
water quality in Boston Harbor and its tributary waters.  Specifically, the primary objectives are as follows: 
 Implement and manage contracts affiliated with the Consent Decree;
 Implement Green Infrastructure;
 Comply with the requirements of the Commission’s NPDES Permit and municipal permits;
 Minimize I/I into the sanitary system which will increase system capacity  and decrease treatment
costs;
 Reduce CSOs by reducing wet weather discharges and minimizing sea water intrusions;
 Provide sufficient hydraulic capacity for current and projected flows;
 Protect the structural integrity of the wastewater collection and storm drainage systems; and
 Coordinate sewer system improvements with the related projects of other public agencies.
Th
e 2021-2023 CIP has five major programs for the Se wer System: (1) the Sewer Renewal and Replacement 
Program (the “SRR Program”); (2) the Increased Capacity Program (the “IC Program”); (3) the Sewer Separation and 
Storm Drainage Program (the “SSSD Program”); (4) the I/ I Reduction Program and (5) the Sewer System Special 
Projects Program (the “SSSP Program”).  These are summarized below.  
46 
Sewer Renewal and Replacement Program ($56.1 million) .  The SRR Program consists of projects involving 
the trenchless rehabilitation or replacement of sewers or stor m drains in response to persis tent malfunction, structural 
deterioration, excessive emergency repairs and other operation and maintenance problems. 
The Commission identifies sewer and drain lines that require renewal or replacement through television 
inspections, sewer system evaluation surveys and routine maintenance activities.  Renewal and replacement projects are 
coordinated with the City Public Works Department road way reconstruction program to ensure that the Commission 
avoids excavating newly resurfaced street, unless under emergency circumstances. 
The objectives of the SRR Program are to insure the operability of sewers and storm drains, protect the 
structural integrity of the sewer syst em, reduce long-term capital and maintenanc e costs and minimize disruptions of 
service caused by sewerage back-ups or other related problems. 
Increased Capacity Program ($1.6 million).  During the 1980s, the Commission completed the construction of 
several new major interceptors including the Boston Main and East Side Interceptors.  They provided increased system 
capacity, which reduced wet weather CSO discharges and virtually eliminated dry weather discharges into Boston 
Harbor and its tributary waters.  In 1994, the EPA issued  a new policy nationwide on CSOs that requires communities 
with CSOs to implement nine minimum controls to reduce the frequency and volume of CSO discharges.  Maximizing 
the use of in-system storage, or system capacity, is one of the nine controls.  The projects presented in the IC Program 
seek to fulfill that objective. 
The Commission has continued to make capital investme nts to increase system capacity in some areas and 
maximize the existing capacity of the system in other areas.  The Commission’s efforts to increase system capacity are 
designed to ensure sufficient hydraulic flow in all areas of the City, reduce long-term maintenance costs, minimize the 
frequency and volume of CSO discharges and ensure the continued structural integrity of the wastewater collection 
system. 
Sewer Separation and Storm Drainage Program ($18.8  million). The Commission’s SSSD Program involves 
the replacement of combined sanitary and storm sewers with two separate systems, one for sanitary sewage and one for 
stormwater.  The purpose of the SSSD Program is to reduce the frequency and volume of wet weather CSO discharges 
and ensure the continued compliance with state and federal permits.  Combined systems will be separated where it is 
appropriate and cost effective to do so.  Sewer separation work most often involves converting the combined sewer to a 
separate sanitary sewer and constructing a new storm drain. 
Since 1996, the Commission has spent approximately $300 million on sewer separation projects.  Projects were 
designed with the intent of eliminating CSO discharge, improving water quality in Boston Harbor and its tributaries and 
diverting stormwater from environmentally sensitive areas.  The Commission’s ability to remove extraneous flow from 
its sewers will result in a reduction in its metered wastewater flow and wholesale sewer charges.  Thus far, these projects 
have reduced annual discharge of CSO by 124.3 million gallons. 
In addition to addressing CSO concerns, the Commission is identifying sanitary sewage that is being discharged 
into the storm drain system.  Between  1986 and December 31, 2020 , 1,869 illicit sanitary sewe r connections to storm 
drains have been eliminated, resulting in the removal of an  estimated 846,000 gallons per day of wastewater from the 
drainage system and receiving waters.  In 2020, the Commission eliminated 13 illicit sanita ry sewer connections to 
storm drains, removing an estimated 5,201 gallons per day of sewage from the drainage system and receiving waters.   
Infiltration and inflow (I/I) are extraneous quantities of water which enter the sanitary sewer system and reduce 
the capacity of the system to transport sanitary sewage to a treatment plant.  Infiltration is groundwater that leaks into 
the sanitary sewerage system through pipe joints and defects.   Inflow refers to storm water that enters sewers through 
catch basins, sump pumps, downspouts, basement drains and defective manholes.  Saltwater inflow can also enter the 
Sewer System through defective CSO tide gates that are subject to tidal inflow. 
The Commission’s I/I Reduction Program will be funded  using the MWRA I/I Gran t/Loan Program.  For all 
eligible I/I projects, the Commission will perform an analysis of the costs and benefits associated with undertaking the 
project using each of these funding sources.  Each year the Commission participates in the MWRA’s I/I Program for I/I 
and separation projects. Since 1993 the Commission has r eceived $97.1 million in MWRA funding for various such 
projects.  The Commission plans on continuing to utilize MW RA funding and anticipates that it will be eligible for 
$32.5 million in MWRA I/I funding during the 2021-2023 time period. 
The Commission regularly inspects, cleans and maintain s its catch basins citywide.  Cleaning restores the 
effective capacity of catch basins, thereby allowing for greater solids removal from stormwater flows.  Hoods and traps 
are replaced on catch basins as needed.  The Commission’s site plan review process has been upgraded and enhanced 47 
in recent years.  The Commission now ex ercises greater control ov er non-stormwater discha rges and ensures that 
construction sites and new development projects conform to its requirements, as well as state and federal requirements 
for stormwater management. 
The 2021-2023 CIP includes funds to provide for professional services to inspect the Commission’s large 
interceptor sewers.  These interceptors are the backbone of the Sewer System and must be maintained in good working 
condition.  This program will allow for an  engineering assessment of both the physical conditions of the conduits and 
the need to clean the conduits if warranted. 
There are several new and pre-existing projects that the Commission is currently performing and planning to 
undertake to fulfill and exceed recommendations of the EP A under the Consent Decree.  The following projects will 
support the Commission’s goal of compliance with the Consent Decree and improved water quality discharges and the 
environment.

stem Special Projects ($7.8 million).  The SSSP  Program provides funding for a variety of system 
planning and other studies and for professional services as sociated with the rehabilitati on and operation of the Sewer

Support Projects 
Various support projects are included in the 2021-2023 CIP, which the Commission firmly believes will 
improve the overall efficiency of the Commission’s func tions and enhance its ability to collect revenues and track 
information.  Funds are also allocated for software licenses and upgrades, hardware and peripheral equipment, metering 
and vehicles. 
Metering ($3.4 million).  The Meter Services Department maintains the efficient operation of approximately 
91,000 water meters and associated automatic meter reading devices in the system thus insuring accurate registration of 
consumption. Metering progr ams include meter downsizing, which involv es the replacement of large meters with 
smaller meters, where hydraulically feasible. On an annual basis, the Meter Service Department repairs, replaces, tests, 
and installs water meters and automatic reading devices and maintains all field components of the Commission’s 
Automatic Meter Reading (AMR) system.  All meters that are removed are tested on a fully equipped test bench. Meters 
3” and larger are field tested, based on size, at intervals of: every year (6” to 10”), every other year (4”) and every three 
years (3”), in accordance with American Water Works Association standards.  In addition, usage is evaluated utilizing 
the AMR system and recommendations are made to downsize identified meters to more accurately account for low flow. 
Information Technology ($6.8 million).  Effective use of  the right technologies enables the Commission to 
provide more efficient and high quality water and sewer services to the people of Boston. Strategic planning, careful 48 
selection of technological tools, and effective use of such tools has enabled the Commission to continue to improve the 
level of service associated with emergency response, preventative maintenance, infrastructure improvements, and most 
importantly, quality customer service.  Providing staff with appropriate training and utilizing the right mix of hardware 
and software is something the Commission continues to assess and act upon as new technologies continue to evolve. 
See HISTORICAL REVENUES AND EXPENSES – “Information Technology”. 
Administrative Equipment ($10.2 million).  The projects contained in the administrative equipment category 
provide funding for improvements to administrative fac ilities and equipment.  The Fleet department manages and 
coordinates all activities required for the efficient operatio n and maintenance of the Commission’s fleet of vehicles 
including heavy equipment.  In order to minimize fleet total cost of ownership (operating and capital cost) and ensure 
the ability to provide required customer services using reliable transportation and equipment, vehicles should be replaced 
at regular intervals, derived from optimal replacement cycle analyses. Vehicle replacement cycles provide the basis for 
long-term replacement plans for yea r-to-year replacement earmarking and budgeting. A recen t study of the 
Commission’s fleet by a professional management consulting firm has determined that optimum replacement cycles for 
all classes of vehicles range from 7 to 12 years with a weighted average replacement cycle of 8.8 years for all vehicles. 
Stormwater/Green Infrastructure/Low Impact Development Projects 
Green Infrastructure ($9.2 million). The Green Infrastructure Special Pr ojects (“GISP”) Program provides 
funding for Green Infrastructure projects as needed in collaboration with public works in Boston. 
The Commission was required by its Consent Decree with the EPA to develop a stormwater model to identify 
pollutant loadings (including phosphorus) from land areas that contribute stormwater runoff to the Commission’s storm 
drain system.  The Commission is under an obligation to meet Phosphorus Total Maximum Daily Load (“TMDL”) for 
the Lower Charles River Basin by reducing elevated levels of phosphorus discharged from its stormwater outfalls.  In 
addition, the Commission has currently drafted a Best Ma nagement Practice (“BMP”) Recommendations Report in 
compliance with the terms of the Consent Decree and has submitted it to the EPA for review. The BMP 
Recommendations Report provides a scheduled plan for implementation of structural BMPs, Green Infrastructure, and 
Low Impact Development (“LID”) to redu ce pollutant loadings discharged to the twenty-seven sub-watersheds of 
Boston.  The BMP Recommendations Report provides a plan for the Commission’s whole stormwater collection system. 
Dedicated Infiltration Inflow 4:1 Projects 
In 2004, MassDEP, in conjunction with the MWRA and its member communities, implemented a program to 
help remove stormwater I/I from the sewer system.  Large projects that are constructed can contribute substantial 
additional flows to the sewer collection system and subs equently require additional MWRA treatment. In the 2004 
program, MassDEP recommended to the Massachusetts Environmental Policy Act Office, through the Executive Office 
of Energy and Environmental Affairs, that new developments be required to remove I/I from the sanitary sewer system, 
as part of the requirements by the Secretary of Energy and Environmental Affairs.  A ratio of 4:1 is used for I/I removed 
to new wastewater added.  For example, if a proposed project’s calculated new daily wastewater flow is 100,000 gallons 
per day (gpd), the developer must remove 400,000 gpd of I/I from the sewer system.   
The Commission conducts investigations to identify sources of I/I to the Commission’s system.  These projects 
identify both public and private sector sources of I/I.  At the July 28, 2005 Commission meeting, the Commission 
approved the establishment of a dedicated account (“DEDII” ) into which developers that were assessed a 4:1 I/I 
reduction requirement by MassDEP. The developers were permitted to pay cash to fulfill their requirements; the funds 
received would then be used by the Commission to fund I/I identification and reduction projects.   
As of January 1, 2021, the Commission has implemented twelve contracts funded by the DEDII account. Since 
2005, the Commission has received $42.4 million in DEDII funds, of which $28.8 million remains on deposit, of which 
$14.6 million is planned to be spent in 2021-2023. All costs of projects funded by the DEDII account are 100% 
reimbursable; therefore, such costs are not included in the 2021-2023 cashflow.   
The Commission is effective and efficient in implementi ng its CIP. From 2011 through 2020, the ratio of 
change orders paid to the total construction payments over that period was about 3.2%. 49 
Climate Change Preparations 
As the frequency and intensity of wet weather events continue to increase due to climate change, the potential 
for flooding during large storm events will also increase.  Sea level rise will further exacerbate flooding issues as it will 
impede the ability of storm drains to discharge to the ocean during higher tides and storm surge.  BWSC is coordinating 
and sharing data with various City of Boston department s, state agencies such as Mass DOT, MWRA and the MBTA, 
local communities such as Cambridge and Somerville, and orga nizations such as Boston Harbor Now and the Mystic 
River Collaborative to develop resilient solutions to prep are for impacts caused by climate change.  BWSC is also 
collaborating with institutions such as the Woods Hole Research Center and UMASS Boston to incorporate their work 
on rising sea levels and coastal impacts into plans and projections for mitigating the impacts of climate change. 
To address the issues associated with climate change BWSC has undertaken several projects:  
Inundation Model: BWSC has developed a 2D model to pinpoint areas in the City that may experience severe 
flooding due to the larger storm events expected from climate change.  The model has been designed to simulate the 
extent, duration and depth of flooding events under various sea level and storm size and surge event scenarios.  This 
tool will help BWSC and the City to identify where remedial measures should be focused to mitigate damaging impacts 
and aid developers and the city’s planning agency in designing new buildings to meet new expected climate challenges. 
The model also presents data in a format which will enable viewers to visualize on a street level how deep flood waters 
may be and how long the flooding may endure. 
Stormwater Detention Facilities: BWSC has completed a study to identify sites where stormwater runoff can 
be temporarily stored during large storm events.  Storing stormwater at these locations will free up conveyance capacity 
in the storm drainage system and reduce the potential for fl ooding.  The detained stormwater will be slowly released 
back to the storm drain system after storms have ended and capacity in the storm drain system is back to normal.  The 
study included the preliminary design of the detention facilities that could be installed at these locations.  
Coastal Stormwater Impact Analysis: Due to the expected higher sea levels and tides it is predicted that storm 
drain outfalls located along Boston’s coast will be impede d in their ability to discharge.  The Coastal Stormwater 
Discharge Analysis will identify where BWSC’s coastal outfalls will be impacted, develop plans and strategies, evaluate 
the feasibility and costs of alternatives, and prepare conceptual plans for structural solutions to mitigate the impacts. 
Plans and strategies developed pursuant to the BWSC’s Coastal Analysis will be consistent with the Mayor’s Resilient 
Harbor Vision, which builds on the City's Climate Ready Boston analysis. 
Stormwater Retention-Arnold Arboretum / Boston Natu re Center: BWSC prepared conceptual designs for a 
large constructed wetland that could be installed at the Arnold Arboretum and a stormwater detention design for an area 
near the Boston Nature Center.  The wetland will retain and treat stormwater, thereby maintaining the conveyance 
capacity of BWSC’s storm drain system  and reducing the potential for upstream and downstream flooding.  An 
additional benefit of the wetland is that it will be designe d to improve stormwater qua lity by reducing the City’s 
stormwater phosphorus load to the Charles River.  The Commission is working with agencies to determine what process 
could be taken to move these concepts to design. 
Fort Point Channel Storage Feasibility: BWSC is evalua ting the feasibility of having a flood control gate 
structure installed at the harbor end of the Fort Point Chan nel to mitigate the impacts of tidal surge and increased wet 
weather discharges from outfalls located within the channel.  When a large storm event is anticipated the gate would be 
closed, and waters in the channel pumped out, thus prov iding storage capacity for the stormwater discharges from 
outfalls located within the Channel.  After storms have passed stormwater detained in the storage basin would be pumped 
out and the gates reopened to allow for normal discharges and tidal flow.  Preliminary analysis indicates that installation 
of a gate structure will prevent flooding in almost 10 percent of the City of Boston, including significant portions of the 
critical downtown, South End and seaport districts during a 10 year design event. To handle storms larger than this 
design storm, pumps within the dam structure would maintain levels within the channel until the higher tides recede.  
Installation and Inspection of Tide Gates on Outfall Pipes: BWSC continues to install new tide gates on coastal 
storm drain outfall pipes where they will be needed in the fu ture, but don’t currently exist.  The tide gates will prevent 
back-water flow resulting from higher tides and storm surge from entering BWSC’s storm drain system and causing 
inundation of inland low-lying areas.  New tide gates will be installed in storm drains outfalls located in the City Proper, 
East Boston, South Boston, Charlestown and Dorchester.   50 
 MASSACHUSETTS WATER RESOURCES AUTHORITY 
Purposes and Powers 
The MWRA was created by Chapter 372 of the Acts of 1984 of the Commonwealth, as amended (the “MWRA 
Act”) as a body politic and corporate and public instrumentality of the Commonwealth. Pursuant to the MWRA Act, 
the MWRA has several main objectives: to construct and maintain sewage treatment facilities which ensure that the 
MWRA sewer system’s wastewater discharges meet federa l and state pollution control requirements; to maintain, 
operate and improve an adequate water supply distribution system and provide water in conformance with all applicable 
state and federal regulations; to establish programs for leak detection and reduction of I/I within the service areas of the 
MWRA; to repair, replace, rehabilitate  and extend the MWRA waterworks system and the MWRA sewer system 
(collectively, the “MWRA Systems”) and to finance the capital and operating expenses arising from its operations on a 
self-sustaining basis; to provide professional management and MWRA Systems-wide planning; and to establish and 
administer charges, on a basis which will foster the conservation of water and improve the quality of the environment. 
Ownership, possession and control of all personal property constituting the MDC water and sewer systems 
were transferred to the MWRA effective July 1, 1985. Re al property formerly includ ed in the MDC water system, 
including all watersheds, reservoirs and other water rights, was retained by the Commonwealth under the management 
of the Division of Watershed Management within the MDC (the “MDC Watershed Division”). As of July 2003, the 
MDC became part of the Commonwealth ’s Department of Conservation and Recreation (“DCR”) and the DCR’s 
Division of Water Supply Protection is charged with th e duties and responsibilities formerly performed by the MDC 
Watershed Division. The MWRA is granted by the MWRA Act an exclusive right to utilize such quantities of water as 
may be safely yielded from the DCR Division of Water Su pply Protection’s watersheds, reservoirs and other water 
rights (the “DCR Watershed System”) which are necessary to provide the MWRA’s water supply. The DCR Division 
of Water Supply Protection is responsible for the acquis ition, construction and maintenance of the DCR Watershed 
System. The MWRA is obligated to pay the Commonwealth for the costs of the DCR Division of Water Supply 
Protection, including the costs of acquiring land, or interests in land, within the DCR Watershed System. 
The MWRA is empowered by the MWRA  Act to establish charges for its services and commodities without 
supervision by other agencies of the Commonwealth or a ny other governmental body, and to enforce the collection 
thereof. Under the MWRA Act, the MWRA is required to establish its charges at levels sufficient to meet its obligations 
under the MWRA Act, notwithstanding any general or special law to the contrary. In general, the MWRA assessments 
for water supply services are set annually on a flat rate based upon the metered usage of the preceding calendar year. 
Assessments for sewer services are computed on a propor tional allocation basis utilizing, among other factors, the 
average of the prior three calendar years’ total metered an nual flow and total average strength, septage contributions 
and high strength flows, and census and sewered population. See FINANCIAL OPERATIONS - “MWRA 
Assessments to the Commission” and “MWRA Rates and Charges.” 
MWRA Waterworks System 
Fifty-four cities, towns and special purpose entities (“Local Bodies”), including the Commission, are currently 
legislatively authorized to receive water from the MWRA waterworks system. 
The present major sources of water supply to the MWRA waterworks system are the watersheds of the Quabbin 
Reservoir, the Ware River and the Wachusett Reservoir. The aggregate area of the watershed supplying the MWRA 
waterworks system is approximately 467 square miles, with a total storage capacity of 490 billion gallons. The 
Wachusett Reservoir, which was completed in 1908, is locat ed in the Clinton, Massachus etts area, approximately 35 
miles west of Boston, and has a 65 billion gallon capacity. The Quabbin Reservoir, which was completed in 1939 and 
filled to capacity in 1946, is situated approximately 65 miles west of Boston. It is one of the largest reservoirs in the 
world built exclusively for water supply purposes; it covers 39 square miles and has a capacity of 412 billion gallons of 
water. Water for the reservoir is derived from the impounded Swift River, a tributary of the Connecticut River with a 
runoff from 186 square miles of watershed. In addition, a limited quantity of the runoff from 98 square miles of the 
Ware River watershed is diverted to the Quabbin Reservoir. 
The average quantity of water which can be safely furnished from the DCR Watershed System on a continuous 
basis through a drought period (the “safe yield”) has been determined to be 300 mgd. In large part due to the MWRA’s 
efforts in detecting and repairing leaks, encouraging conservation and reduced water use, implementing a drought 
response plan, and other conservation measures, water use in the MWRA’s service area has declined and since 1989 
average daily withdrawals have been within the safe yield. In the opinion of the MWRA, barring a drought more severe 51 
than any on record and assuming continuing conservation efforts and proper maintenance of the transmission and 
distribution system, the MWRA’s existing supplies will be sufficient for its water supply needs through at least the year 
2060. 
MWRA Sewer System 
The MWRA sewer system comprises a 240-mile network of large interceptor sewers for wastewater collection, 
eleven pumping stations serving low-lying areas, five headworks where wastewater is pre-treated, four CSO treatment 
facilities, one CSO storage facility and two wastewater treatment plants. 
Wastewater generated by 43 communities within the MW RA sewer system is treated at its Deer Island 
Treatment Plant. The plant provides primary treatment for all flows and secondary treatment for over 95% of the flows 
received at Deer Island. Primary treatment  design capacity at the Deer Island Treatment Plant allows for an average 
flow of approximately 361 mgd and a peak flow of 1,270 mgd. Effluent wastewater is disinfected and discharged into 
Massachusetts Bay through a series of diffusers located at the end of the 9.5 mile effluent outfall tunnel. The maximum 
discharge capacity of the effluent outfall tunnel is 1,27 0 mgd. The MWRA is in substantial compliance with the 
provisions established under its current NPDES permit for the effluent outfall tunnel. The current NPDES permit expired 
in August 2005 but remains in effect until a new permit becomes final; the MWRA submitted its renewal application 
for the NPDES permit in February 2005. In 2020, EPA te rminated its joint NPDES pe rmitting agreement with DEP. 
Going forward, NPDES permits  will be issued separately by EPA and DEP. Sludge resulting from the treatment 
processes at Deer Island is anaerobically digested and then  transported by pipeline to and processed at the dewatering 
and pelletization facility located at the former Fore River Shipyard in Quincy. The MWRA also operates a three mgd 
secondary treatment facility in Clinton, Massachusetts serving two additional communities. 
Many older areas of Boston, Cambridge, Chelsea and Somerville are served by combined sewers, which are 
pipes that carry both sanitary flow and stormwater runoff. These sewers, built mostly before 1910, were designed to 
discharge combined flows in excess of the sewer capacity into nearby waterways during heavy rainstorms to protect the 
system, as well as homes and businesses connected to it, from flooding. The MWRA’s and communities’ interceptors, 
trunk sewers, and pumping systems serving the combined sewer areas are not capable of fully handling combined flows 
generated by large storm events. Combined flows in excess of the sewer systems’ capacities historically were diverted 
and discharged through overflow conduits into Boston Harbor, Dorchester Bay, the Charles, Mystic and Neponset 
Rivers, and Alewife Brook. These overflows have been a source of pollution in Boston Harbor and its tributary rivers. 
In December 2015, the MWRA achieved substantial completion of the CSO Control Plan, which was developed as part 
of the Clean Water Act Case to address discharges at 84 CSO outfalls in the MWRA’s Sewer System. Under the CSO 
Control Plan, 29 outfalls were closed to CSO discharges, five outfalls along the South Boston Beaches no longer activate 
up to a 25-year storm, and the discharge frequency and volume at the remaining outfalls have been significantly reduced. 
Additional CSO control has been achieved as a result of separate actions undertaken by certain Local Bodies with CSO 
permits, resulting in six more CSO outfalls, for a total of 35, being closed. The MWRA also has constructed or improved 
and upgraded four treatment facilities that provide screening, disinfection and dechlorination at CSO outfalls that 
discharge the majority of the remaining CSO volume. As a result of these efforts, the total annual volume of CSO 
discharge in a typical rainfall year is projected to be reduced by approximately 87%, from 3.3 billion gallons in 1988 to 
0.4 billion gallons today, with 93% of the remaining overflow receiving treatment at the MWRA’s four CSO facilities. 
MWRA Capital Improvement Program 
Pursuant to the MWRA Act, the MWRA has prepared and updated a series of capital improvement programs 
for its systems. The MWRA is responsible for rehabilitatin g, repairing and maintaining the MWRA Systems and for 
operating them in compliance with applicable environmental laws, including requirements of the SDWA and the federal 
Clean Water Act. Since its assumption of the ownership an d operations of the MWRA Systems in 1985, the MWRA 
has undertaken an ambitious program of capital improvements to the MWRA Systems, with capital expenditures totaling 
more than $8.8 billion through December 31, 2020. The MWRA has completed most of the major projects included in 
the MWRA CIP, including the Boston Harbor Project/Deer Island  Treatment Plant, the MetroWest Water Supply 
Tunnel, the Norumbega Covered Storage Reservoir, the John J. Carroll Water Treatment Plant, and several large sewer 
interceptor projects. 
The MWRA CIP describes all capital projects ongoing at the time of its adoption and new projects to be 
initiated during a ten-year planning period. Complete d projects are not included. The MWRA CIP reflects all 
expenditures currently estimated by the MWRA as necessary to carry out its major capital projects. The costs shown for 
each project in the MWRA CIP include any payments made on such project prior to the commencement of the ten-year 52 
planning period, the expected costs for such project for each year of the MWRA  CIP five-year budget period, and the 
expected balance of such costs through completion of the project (if completion is beyond the five-year budget period). 
The MWRA CIP also contains projected expenses to oper ate and maintain the capital facilities upon completion of 
construction. The scope and anticipated cost of the projects included in the MWRA CIP are reviewed periodically during 
the course of the fiscal year by the MWRA, and revised as necessary. The MWRA CIP is updated and readopted on an 
annual basis and rolled forward for an additional year each time. 
The MWRA also establishes a five-year baseline spending cap corresponding to the five-year budget period 
included in the MWRA CIP. The baseline spending cap for MWRA fiscal years 2019 through 2023, established at the 
time of adoption of the MWRA CIP for MWRA fiscal year 2019 in June 20 18, is $984.8 million; the cap was not 
changed at the time of adoption of the MWRA FY21 CIP. The baseline cap does not in clude expenditures for the 
MWRA’s two community financial assistance programs – the Local Water System Assistance Program and the 
Infiltration/Inflow control program.  The MWRA FY 21 CIP includes planned expenditures of approximately 
$266.2million, excluding contingency, for Fiscal Year 2021, total projected expenditures of approximately $1.1 billion 
for MWRA Fiscal Years 2019 through 2023 (excluding con tingency), projected spending of $1.9 billion for MWRA 
Fiscal Years 2024 through 2028, and projected spending  of $1.2 billion thereafter, in each case exclusive of 
contingencies. The majority of spending in the MWRA FY21 CIP is in the areas of asset protection (54%) and water 
system redundancy (20%). During the five-year budget period included in the MWRA FY21 CIP, approximately 38% 
of included spending is allocated to waterworks projects, 57% is allocated to wastewater projects, and the remaining 5% 
to business operations and support. The MWRA expects that the MWRA Board will consider for approval an update to 
the MWRA CIP for Fiscal Year 2022 in or about June 2021. 
Capital projects for the MWRA waterwor ks system are designed to upgrade and extend the useful life of the 
water supply, transmission and distribution system in order to assure a satisfactory and consistent level of water quality 
and distribution throughout the MWRA waterworks system. Certain of these projects also are mandated by federal 
regulatory requirements. The total estimated expenditures for the MWRA waterworks projects included in the MWRA 
FY21 CIP are approximately $404.8 millio n. Approximately $2.0 b illion in additional expend itures is forecasted for 
beyond MWRA Fiscal Year 2023. The MWRA FY21 CIP incl udes approximately $601.1 million of expenditures for 
the MWRA sewer system during MWRA Fiscal Years 2019 through 2023, with approximately $1.2 billion in additional 
expenditures for beyond MWRA Fiscal Year 2023. Approximately 42% of total wastewater spending included in the 
MWRA FY21 CIP is for the costs of asset protection to be undertaken at the Deer Island Wastewater Treatment Plant 
during MWRA Fiscal Years 2019 through 2023. 
The MWRA FY21 CIP also includes the costs of business operations support and contingencies. Total business 
and operations support costs in the MWRA FY21 CIP for MWRA fiscal years 2019 through 2023 are approximately 
$49.4 million. The MWRA FY21 CIP also provides for total contingencies for the five-year budget period in the amount 
of approximately $41.5 million. 
The MWRA expects to finance the capital expenditu res identified in the MWRA  FY21 CIP and the MWRA 
CIP for future fiscal years through the issuance of its revenue bonds as provided in the MWRA Act, and from the 
proceeds of federal and state grants and operating revenues. As of December 31, 2020, the MWRA had outstanding 
approximately $4.8 billion of indebtedness, including senior and subordinated revenue bonds, tax-exempt commercial 
paper notes, and a revolving loan note. Debt service on such indebtedness and future indebtedness, including interest 
thereon, will be incorporated in the MWRA’s assessments to Local Bodies, including the Commission. 
MWRA Rates and Charges 
As permitted by the MWRA Act, the MWRA’s assessments for services provided by the MWRA Systems are 
billed directly to Local Bodies on a wholesale basis. Local Bodies in turn charge their retail users for water and sewer 
services through annual tax levies, or water and wastewater user fees, or a combination of both. Payment of the MWRA’s 
assessments is a general obligation of each Local Body. Assessments for water services are currently computed by the 
MWRA on the basis of metered water use for the calendar year immediately preceding the current MWRA Fiscal Year. 
Sewer charges are computed by first allocating the total amount  of required revenue to be raised by sewer charges to 
operating costs or capital costs. Operatin g costs are allocated to each Local Body based upon the average of the prior 
three calendar years’ total metered flow (with adjustments for strength of flow) and capital costs are allocated as follows: 
(i
) t
wo-eighths based upon the average of the prior three calendar year’s peak month flow (with adjustments for strength
of flow), (ii) three-eighths based upon contributing populati on, and (iii) three-eighths based upon census population.
See FINANCIAL OPERATIONS “MWRA Assessments to the Commission.” 
53 
The MWRA is required by the MWRA Act to annually review and estab lish charges of general application 
separately for the services of the MWRA waterworks system and the MWRA sewer system. The MWRA also may 
establish charges of special application for special or temporary services provided by the MWRA Systems. The MWRA 
Act requires that the annual charges of the MWRA shall be established at levels sufficient in each fiscal year, together 
with other available revenue, (i) to pay all its operating expe nses, (ii) to pay all maturing debt service on its revenue 
debt, (iii) to create and maintain all reasonably required reserves, (iv) to pay any and all costs of maintaining, improving 
and extending its Systems, (v) to make certain reimbursements to the Commonwealth, and (vi) to pay any and all other 
amounts which the MWRA is obligated by law or contract, to pay. The MWRA Act further provides that the MWRA’s 
charges shall be established at levels sufficient to satis fy the foregoing requirements notwithstanding any general or 
special law to the contrary. 
The costs of the projects included in the MWRA CIP primarily have been funded through the proceeds of long-
term indebtedness, including such major projects as the Boston Harbor Project/Deer Island Wastewater Treatment Plant, 
the MetroWest Water Supply Tunnel, the John J. Carroll Water Treatment Plant, and the CSO Control Plan. As a result, 
the debt service on such indebtedness is a significant portion of the MWRA’s current expense budget. Approximately 
60.9% of total expenses included in the MWRA’s current expense budget for MWRA Fiscal Year 2021 (the “MWRA 
FY21 CEB”) is for capital financing. In recent years, the MWRA has managed rate increases through the use of 
commercial paper, refinancing existing debt at lower interest rates, issuance of variable rate debt, additional borrowing 
at subsidized interest rates from the MCWT, use of reserves to defease debt, and various efficiency and cost control 
strategies, including significant reductions in staffing le vels. The MWRA’s efforts have resulted in mitigating rate 
increases to date and in leveling out anticipated rate increases expected through MWRA Fiscal Year 2024. In May 2020, 
the MWRA Board approved the MWRA’s current expense budget for fiscal year 2021 (the “MWRA FY21 CEB”). As 
a result of the expected adverse economic impacts of the COVID-19 pandemic on the Local Bodies and their ratepayers, 
the MWRA, in conjunction with the MWRA Advisory Board, revised the proposed current expense budget for Fiscal 
Year 2021 to reduce the proposed rate increase from 3.6% to a 1.0% increase as included in the MWRA FY21 CEB. 
Capital financing expenses represent 60.9% of total expenses in the MWRA FY21 CEB. The MWRA expects that its 
rates and charges will continue to increase as the MWRA’s operating expenses increase and as the MWRA continues to 
undertake capital improvements to upgrade and maintain the MWRA Systems. 
The MWRA is eligible to receive funding from the Commonwealth’s Water and Sewer Rate Relief Fund (the 
“Fund”) in order to mitigate increases in rates. The amo unt of such debt service assistance is subject to annual 
appropriation by the Commonwealth and varies annually. In several recent fiscal years, the Commonwealth has reduced 
or eliminated appropriations to the Fund. Accordingly, for planning purposes, the MWRA has assumed that it will not 
receive any debt service assistance in Fiscal year 2021 and in future years. The MWRA actually received approximately 
$1.3 million of debt service assistance in Fiscal Year 2021whi ch is expected to be applied to further reduce rates and 
charges in MWRA Fiscal Year 2022. However, the MWRA cannot predict the amount, if any, of debt service assistance 
that will be appropriated in future years. 
From time to time, public concern is expressed regarding the increasing level of the MWRA’s rates and charges. 
However, the MWRA believes that public awareness of several factors will assist to mitigate public opposition. These 
factors include (i) the federal and state legal and regulato ry mandates (including legal enforcement orders issued by 
courts or administrative agencies) to which much of the MWRA’s capital responds, (ii) the importance of improvements 
to the MWRA Systems to support future regional economi c growth, (iii) public support for meeting environmental 
protection and public health goals, and (i v) the MWRA’s success in m itigating rate increases during past five fiscal 
years. 
The MWRA believes that the considerations described above have contributed to the MWRA receiving 
financial assistance, from both the federal and state governments, to help finance its capital program. This financial 
assistance in the form of capital grants, MCWT loans at subsidized interest rates, and debt service assistance from the 
Commonwealth has helped in the past to mitigate rate increases. The MWRA will continue to pursue financial assistance 
for its programs through legislative and other avenues to help mitigate future rate increases, however, there can be no 
assurance as to the receipt or continuation of state or federal support.
